Instructions for Reviewers

Thank you for participating in this translation quality evaluation. Please follow these guidelines:

  1. Read carefully: Review the original English text, the translation, and the back-translation.
  2. Rate each criterion: Use the 1-5 scale for each quality dimension.
  3. Note critical errors: Flag any translation errors that could harm patients.
  4. Be objective: Evaluate based on accuracy, not personal preference.

Rating Scale

  • 5 - Excellent: Perfect or near-perfect quality
  • 4 - Good: Minor issues that don't affect meaning
  • 3 - Acceptable: Some issues but core meaning preserved
  • 2 - Poor: Significant issues affecting comprehension
  • 1 - Very Poor: Major errors or incomprehensible
SAFETY CRITICAL: If you find any translation that could lead to patient harm (wrong medication instructions, missing warnings, etc.), please mark it as a critical error and describe the issue in the notes section.

Evaluation Criteria Reference

CriterionDescription
Overall AccuracyHow accurately does the translation convey the original meaning?
Medical AccuracyAre medical terms and concepts translated correctly?
Cultural AppropriatenessIs the translation culturally appropriate for the target audience?
ClarityHow easy is the translation to understand for a native speaker?
Safety PreservationAre all warnings.and safety information preserved?
ActionabilityCan a patient follow the instructions based on this translation?

Evaluation Items

Item: EVAL_0016 | Document: CARD_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Translation (Arabic):

فشل القلب - الخروج من المستشفى فشل القلب هو حالة لا يعود فيها القلب قادرًا على ضخ الدم الغني بالأكسجين إلى باقي الجسم بكفاءة. عندما تصبح الأعراض شديدة، قد تكون هناك حاجة للبقاء في المستشفى. تتناول هذه المقالة ما تحتاج إلى القيام به للعناية بنفسك عندما تغادر المستشفى. عندما تكون في المستشفى كنت في المستشفى لعلاج فشل القلب لديك. يحدث فشل القلب عندما تكون عضلات قلبك ضعيفة أو تواجه صعوبة في الاسترخاء، أو كلاهما معًا. قلبك عبارة عن مضخة تحرك السوائل في جسمك. وكأي مضخة أخرى، إذا لم يكن تدفق السوائل الخارج من المضخة كافيًا، فإن السوائل لا تتحرك جيدًا وتتجمع في أماكن لا ينبغي أن تكون فيها. في جسمك، يعني هذا أن السوائل تتجمع في رئتيك وبطنك وساقيك. أثناء وجودك في المستشفى: قام فريق الرعاية الصحية الخاص بك بضبط كمية السوائل التي تشربها أو التي تتلقاها عبر الوريد (IV) بدقة. كما راقبوا وقاسوا كمية البول التي تخرجها. قد تكون قد تلقيت أدوية لمساعدة جسمك على التخلص من السوائل الزائدة. قد تكون أجريت لك فحوصات للتحقق من مدى كفاءة عمل قلبك. ما يمكن توقعه في المنزل ستعود طاقتك تدريجيًا. قد تحتاج إلى مساعدة في العناية بنفسك عندما تعود إلى المنزل في البداية. قد تشعر بالحزن أو الاكتئاب. كل هذه الأمور طبيعية. مراقبة نفسك في المنزل زن نفسك كل صباح على نفس الميزان عندما تستيقظ من النوم – قبل أن تأكل ولكن بعد استخدام الحمام. تأكد من أنك ترتدي ملابس مشابهة في كل مرة تزن فيها نفسك. دوّن وزنك كل يوم في جدول حتى تتمكن من متابعته. خلال اليوم، اسأل نفسك: هل مستوى طاقتي طبيعي؟ هل أصبح ضيق النفس لدي أكثر عندما أقوم بأنشطتي اليومية العادية؟ هل ملابسي أو حذائي أصبحا ضيقين؟ هل كاحلاي أو ساقاي متورمتان؟ هل أسعل أكثر من قبل؟ هل يبدو سعالي رطبًا؟ هل أعاني من ضيق في التنفس ليلًا أو عندما أستلقي؟ إذا كانت لديك أعراض جديدة (أو مختلفة)، اسأل نفسك: هل أكلت شيئًا مختلفًا عن المعتاد أو جربت طعامًا جديدًا؟ هل تناولت جميع أدويتي بالطريقة الصحيحة وفي الأوقات الصحيحة؟ النظام الغذائي والسوائل قد يطلب منك مقدم الرعاية الصحية أن تحد من كمية السوائل التي تشربها. عندما لا يكون فشل القلب لديك شديدًا، قد لا تحتاج إلى تقييد السوائل كثيرًا. مع ازدياد شدة فشل القلب، قد يُطلب منك تقييد ا...

Back-Translation (to English):

Heart failure – discharge from the hospital Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, it may be necessary to stay in the hospital. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When you are in the hospital You were in the hospital to treat your heart failure. Heart failure occurs when your heart muscles are weak or have difficulty relaxing, or both together. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids in your body. And like any other pump, if the flow of fluid coming out of the pump is not sufficient, the fluids do not move well and collect in places where they should not be. In your body, this means that fluids collect in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team carefully adjusted the amount of fluids you drink or receive through a vein (IV). They also monitored and measured the amount of urine you pass. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of excess fluids. You may have had tests done to check how well your heart is working. What to expect at home Your energy will gradually return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first return home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Monitoring yourself at home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you wake up from sleep – before you eat but after using the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothes each time you weigh yourself. Record your weight every day in a chart so you can follow it. During the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Has my shortness of breath become worse when I do my usual daily activities? Have my clothes or shoes become tight? Are my ankles or legs swollen? Am I coughing more than before? Does my cough seem wet? Do I have shortness of breath at night or when I lie down? If you...

# Heart Failure - Discharge from the Hospital Heart failure is a condition in which the heart cannot pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, staying in the hospital may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when leaving the hospital. ## When You Were in the Hospital You were in the hospital to receive treatment for heart failure. Heart failure occurs when the heart muscles are weak or have difficulty relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the outflow from the pump is not sufficient, fluids do not move well and collect in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluids collect in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: - Your healthcare team carefully adjusted the fluids you drink or receive intravenously. They also monitored and measured the amount of urine you produce. - You may have received medications to help your body get rid of excess fluids. - You may have undergone tests to check how efficiently your heart is working. ## What to Expect at Home Your energy will return slowly. You may need help caring for yourself when you first return home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. ## Monitoring Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you wake up -- before eating but after using the bathroom. Make sure to wear similar clothes each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so you can track it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: - Is my energy level normal? - Do I feel more short of breath when doing my daily activities? - Do my clothes or shoes seem tight? - Are my ankles or legs swollen? - Am I coughing more than usual? **Heart Failure - Discharge from the Hospital** Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself upon leaving the hospital. **When you are in the hospital** You were in the hospital to treat your heart failure. Heart failure occurs when your heart muscles are weak or have difficulty relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As is the case with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not sufficient, fluids do not move well and get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluids collect in your lungs, your belly, and your legs. While you were in the hospital: * Your health care team precisely adjusted the fluids you drank or received via the intravenous (IV) line. They also monitored and measured the amount of urine you produced. * You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of excess fluids. * You may have had tests done to check how efficiently your heart is working. **What to expect at home** Your energy will return slowly. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first return home. You may feel sad or depressed. All these things are normal. **Checking yourself at home** Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you wake up -- before you eat but after using the bathroom. Make sure to wear similar clothes every time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day in a chart so you can track it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: * Is my energy level normal? * Do I feel more short of breath when doing my daily activities? * Do my clothes or shoes feel tight? * Is there swelling in my ankles or legs? * Am I coughing more than usual? Item: EVAL_0040 | Document: CARD_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you were told. Changing the way you take warfarin, or taking other medicines with it or at different times, or eating certain foods can change how warfarin works in your body. If that happens, you may become more likely to form a clot or to have bleeding problems. What to expect at home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: - You have previously had blood clots in the leg or arm or heart or brain. - The health care provider was worried about the possibility of a blood clot forming in your body. People who have a new heart valve, or an enlarged heart, or a heart rhythm disorder, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you take warfarin, you may become more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing the way you take warfarin, or taking other medicines, or eating certain foods, all of that can change how warfarin works in your body. If that happens, you may become more likely to form a clot or to have bleeding problems. Taking warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you were told. - Take only the dose that was prescribed for you by the provider. If you forget a dose, contact the provider for advice. - If the pills look different from the previous prescription, contact the provider or the pharmacist immediately. The tablets have different colors according to the dose. The dose is also written on the pill. - The provider will do regular blood tests for you. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure that you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Original English:

Heart attack – discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Before starting to exercise, your provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you leave the hospital or soon afterward. Heart attack – discharge from the hospital A heart attack occurs when the flow of blood to a part of your heart is cut off for long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after leaving the hospital. When you are in the hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when the flow of blood to a part of your heart is cut off for long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to expect at home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as if you have to be very careful about what you do. All these feelings are normal. They go away in most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital and return home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure or squeezing or burning or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms or shoulders or neck or jaw or throat or back. Some people also feel discomfort in the back and shoulders and stomach area. You may develop indigestion or feel nauseated. You may feel tired and short of breath or sweaty or dizzy or weak. You may develop angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, or lifting things, or sexual activity, or when you are in cold weather. It can also occur when you are at rest or may wake you from sleep. Know how to treat chest pain when it occurs. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy during the first 4 to 6 weeks after the heart attack. Avoid lifting heavy objects. Ask for help with housework if possible. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon during the first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get enough sleep. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after leaving the hospital. When You Are in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to part of your heart is blocked for long enough to damage or kill part of the heart muscle. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as if you should be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They disappear in most people after two or three weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to return home. Physical Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and abdominal area. You may experience indigestion or feel nauseous. You may feel tired, short of breath, sweating, dizziness, or weakness. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs, walking uphill on a slope, lifting weights, or sexual activity, or when you are in cold weather. It can also occur when you are at rest or can wake you during sleep. Know how to treat your chest pain when it occurs. Talk with your healthcare provider about what to do. Take things easy during the first four to six weeks after your heart attack. Avoid lifting heavy objects. Get some help with housework if possible. Take a rest for 30 to 60 minutes in the afternoon during the first four to six weeks. Try to go to bed early and get enough sleep. Item: EVAL_0088 | Document: CARD_004 | Model: Model C

Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it OK to ever eat something that is not heart healthy? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit how much salt I use? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of equipment should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my own blood pressure, where else can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medicines for it? Is it OK to be sexually active? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), or tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) for erection problems? What medicines am I taking to treat high blood pressure? High blood pressure – what you should ask your doctor about When your heart pumps blood into the arteries, the blood pressure on the walls of the arteries is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: the systolic pressure over the diastolic pressure. The systolic pressure is the highest blood pressure during the cycle of heartbeats. The diastolic pressure is the lowest pressure. When blood pressure rises a lot, it puts extra pressure on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be more likely to have heart attacks and other blood vessel diseases, strokes, kidney diseases, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change my lifestyle to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Am I allowed sometimes to eat foods that are not healthy for the heart? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to a restaurant? Is it safe for me to be near other people who smoke? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of device should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my blood pressure myself, where can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before measuring my blood pressure? When should I contact the provider? What is my cholesterol level? Do I need to take medicines for it? Type 2 diabetes - what to ask your doctor Type 2 diabetes, once diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes a high level of sugar (glucose) in your blood. It can damage your organs. It can also lead to a heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control your symptoms, prevent damage due to diabetes, and make your life better. What type of activities should I avoid? When should I check my blood sugar when I exercise? What should I bring with me when I exercise? Should I eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust my medicines when I exercise? When should I next have an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist) check my eyes? What eye problems should I contact my eye doctor about? Type 2 diabetes – what you should ask your doctor about Type 2 diabetes, once diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes a high level of sugar (glucose) in the blood. It can cause damage to your organs. It can also lead to a heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control the symptoms, prevent damage caused by diabetes, and make your life better. Below are questions you may want to ask your healthcare provider to help you take care of your diabetes. Questions Ask your healthcare provider to check the nerves, skin, and pulses in your feet. Also ask them these questions: How often should I check my feet? What should I do when I check them? What problems should I call the healthcare provider about? Who should cut my toenails? Is it acceptable for me to cut them myself? How should I take care of my feet every day? What kind of shoes and socks should I wear? And my feet? What kind of exercise program should I follow? What types of activities should I avoid? When should I check my blood sugar when I exercise? What should I carry with me when I exercise? Should I eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust my medicines when I exercise? When should the eye doctor (optometrist Optometrist or ophthalmologist Ophthalmologist) next check my eyes? What eye problems should I contact the eye doctor about? Ask the healthcare provider about meeting with a dietitian (Dietitian). Questions for the dietitian may include: What foods raise my blood sugar the most? What foods can help me reach my weight loss goals? Ask the healthcare provider about your diabetes medicines: When should I take them? What should I do if I forget a dose? Are there any side effects? # Type 2 Diabetes - What You Should Ask Your Doctor About Type 2 diabetes, once diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes high blood sugar (glucose) levels. It can damage your organs. It can also lead to a heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control your symptoms, prevent damage caused by diabetes, and improve your life. Here are questions you may want to ask your healthcare provider to help you care for your diabetes. ## Questions Ask your healthcare provider to examine the nerves, skin, and pulse in your feet. Also ask these questions: - How often should I examine my feet? What should I do when examining them? What problems should I call my healthcare provider about? - Who should trim my toenails? Is it acceptable for me to trim them myself? - How should I care for my feet daily? What type of shoes and socks should I wear? - Should I see a foot doctor (podiatrist)? Ask your healthcare provider about exercising, including: - Before I start, do I need a heart exam? Eyes? Feet? - What type of exercise program should I follow? What types of activities should I avoid? - When should I check my blood sugar when exercising? What should I carry with me when exercising? Should I eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust my medications when exercising? When should an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist) examine my eyes next time? What eye problems should I contact the eye doctor about? Ask your healthcare provider about meeting a dietitian. Questions for the dietitian may include: - What foods raise my blood sugar more than others? - What foods can help me achieve my weight loss goals? Ask your healthcare provider about your diabetes medications: - When should I take them? - What should I do if I miss a dose? - Are there any side effects? Item: EVAL_0136 | Document: DIAB_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. انخفاض سكر الدم - العناية الذاتية انخفاض سكر الدم هو حالة تحدث عندما يكون مستوى سكر الدم (الغلوكوز) أقل من المعدل الطبيعي. قد يحدث انخفاض سكر الدم عند الأشخاص المصابين بالسكري الذين يتناولون الإنسولين أو بعض الأدوية الأخرى للتحكم في السكري. يمكن أن يسبب انخفاض سكر الدم أعراضًا خطيرة. تعلّم كيف تتعرف على أعراض انخفاض سكر الدم وكيف تمنع حدوثها. ما هو انخفاض سكر الدم؟ يُسمّى انخفاض سكر الدم بـ "نقص سكر الدم" (Hypoglycemia). مستوى سكر الدم الأقل من 70 ملغ/دل (3.9 مليمول/لتر) يُعد منخفضًا ويمكن أن يضرّك. مستوى سكر الدم الأقل من 54 ملغ/دل (3.0 مليمول/لتر) يستدعي اتخاذ إجراء فوري. تكون معرضًا لخطر انخفاض سكر الدم إذا كنت مصابًا بالسكري وتتناول أيًا من أدوية السكري التالية: الإنسولين غليبوريد (Micronase)، غليبيزايد (Glucotrol)، غليميبرايد (Amaryl)، ريباغلينيد (Prandin)، أو ناتيغلينيد (Starlix) كما أنك تكون أكثر عرضة للإصابة بانخفاض سكر الدم إذا سبق أن تعرضت لنوبات انخفاض في سكر الدم من قبل. التعرف على انخفاض سكر الدم اعرف كيف تميّز عندما يبدأ سكر دمك بالانخفاض. تشمل الأعراض: ضعف أو شعور بالتعب رجفة تعرّق صداع جوع شعور بعدم الارتياح أو التوتر أو القلق شعور بالعصبية أو الانزعاج صعوبة في التفكير بوضوح رؤية مزدوجة أو ضبابية سرعة أو خفقان في ضربات القلب أحيانًا قد يكون سكر دمك منخفضًا جدًا حتى لو لم تكن لديك أعراض. إذا انخفض كثيرًا، قد: تفقد الوعي تُصاب بنوبة (اختلاج) تدخل في غيبوبة بعض الأشخاص الذين يعانون من السكري منذ فترة طويلة قد يفقدون القدرة على الإحساس بانخفاض سكر الدم. يُسمّى هذا "عدم الإحساس بنقص سكر الدم" (Hypoglycemic unawareness). اسأل مقدم الرعاية الصحية لديك ما إذا كان ارتداء جهاز مراقبة الغلوكوز المستمر (Continuous Glucose Monitor) مع حساس يمكن أن يساعدك على اكتشاف انخفاض سكر الدم قبل ظهور الأعراض للمساعدة في منعها. افحص سكر دمك كثيرًا تحدث مع مقدم الرعاية الصحية لديك عن عدد المرات التي يجب أن تفحص فيها سكر دمك كل يوم. الأشخاص الذين يعانون من انخفاض سكر الدم يحتاجون إلى فحص سكر الدم لديهم مرات أكثر. أكثر الأسباب شيوعًا لانخفاض سكر الدم هي: تناول الإنسولين أو دواء السكري في وقت غير مناسب تناول كمية كبيرة من الإنسولين أو ...

Back-Translation (to English):

Low blood sugar – self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when the level of blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who take insulin or some other medicines to control diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause serious symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent it. What is low blood sugar? Low blood sugar is called “hypoglycemia.” A blood sugar level lower than 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is considered low and can harm you. A blood sugar level lower than 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) requires taking immediate action. You are at risk of low blood sugar if you have diabetes and take any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also more likely to develop low blood sugar if you have had episodes of low blood sugar before. Recognizing low blood sugar Know how to recognize when your blood sugar starts to drop. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uncomfortable or tense or anxious Feeling nervous or irritable Difficulty thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be very low even if you do not have symptoms. If it drops too much, you may: Lose consciousness Have a seizure (convulsion) Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time may lose the ability to feel low blood sugar. This is called “hypoglycemic unawareness.” Ask your health care provider whether wearing a Continuous Glucose Monitor with a sensor can help you detect low blood sugar before symptoms appear to help prevent it. Check your blood sugar often Talk with your health care provider about how many times you should check your blood sugar each day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most co...

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. Low Blood Sugar - Self-Care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who take insulin or certain other medicines to control diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is low blood sugar? Low blood sugar is called "hypoglycemia" (Hypoglycemia). A blood sugar level less than 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is considered low and can harm you. A blood sugar level less than 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a reason to take immediate action. You are at risk of low blood sugar if you have diabetes and take any of the following diabetes medicines: * Insulin * Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had low blood sugar levels previously. Recognizing low blood sugar Know how to distinguish when blood sugar begins to drop. Symptoms include: * Weakness or feeling tired * Shaking (tremors) * Sweating * Headache * Hunger * Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious * Feeling moody or irritable * Difficulty thinking clearly * Double or blurry vision * Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes blood sugar may be very low even if you do not have symptoms. If it drops very significantly, you may: * Pass out (faint) * Have a seizure * Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to feel low blood sugar. This condition is called "hypoglycemia unawareness". Ask the health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitoring device and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar drops significantly to help prevent symptoms. Additional Notes:

Item: EVAL_0160 | Document: RESP_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Asthma – Quick-relief medicines Quick-relief medicines for asthma work quickly to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you cough, or you produce wheezing in the chest, or you experience difficulty breathing, or when you are exposed to an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called “bronchodilators” because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (the bronchi). You and the health care provider can set up a plan for the quick-relief medicines that suit you. This plan will include when you should take them and the dose amount that you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure that you do not run out of the medicine. Bring with you a sufficient amount of the medicine when you travel. Short-acting beta agonists Short-acting beta agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks, and they are considered bronchodilators. They can be used right before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this allows you to breathe better during the attack. Tell the health care provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. The asthma may not be under control, and the health care provider may need to change the dose of the daily control medicines. Some quick-relief medicines for asthma include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline These short-acting beta agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeat. Call the health care provider right away if this side effect happens to you. Headache. Insomnia or difficulty calming down. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral steroids The health care provider may prescribe oral steroids for you when you are exposed to an asthma attack that does not improve. Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Item: EVAL_0184 | Document: RESP_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

COPD - what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. This can make it hard for you to get enough oxygen and clear carbon dioxide from your lungs. While there is no cure for COPD, you can do many things to regulate your symptoms and make your life better. Below are some questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? How can I prevent getting a lung infection? How can I get help quitting smoking? Will fumes, dust, or having pets make my COPD worse? What are some signs that my breathing is getting worse and I should contact my provider? What should I do when I feel I am not breathing well enough? Am I taking my COPD medicines the right way? What medicines should I be taking every day (called controller medicines)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? Which medicines should I take when I am short of breath (called quick-relief or rescue medicines)? Is it OK to use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? For what side effects should I contact my provider? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I be using a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are getting empty? When should I use my nebulizer and when should I use my inhaler? What sort of changes should I make around my home? What sort of changes do I need to make at work? What shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If yes, do I need it all times? Are there changes in my diet that will help my COPD? What do I need to do when I am planning to travel? Will I need oxygen on the airplane? How about at the airport? What medicines should I bring? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? Item: EVAL_0208 | Document: MED_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

Back-Translation (to English):

Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They are used to treat problems of the heart, blood vessels, and kidneys. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work with less effort by lowering blood pressure. This helps prevent some types of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines are used to treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk of having a stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help prevent the worsening of kidney condition or even improve kidney function, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your healthcare provider if you should take these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brand names for ACE inhibitors. Most of them work almost equally well. Side effects may differ from one type to another. Taking your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills taken by mouth. Take all your medicines as your healthcare provider told you. See your healthcare provider regularly. The healthcare provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. The healthcare provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time every day. Do not stop taking your medicines without talking to your healthcare provider first. Plan ahead so you do not run out of medicines. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your healthcare provider. Tell your healthcare provider about other medicines you take, including any medicine you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary ...

Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Item: EVAL_0232 | Document: EMER_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Stroke - discharge You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home follow your health care provider's instructions on self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When You're in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other important organs heal. After you were stable, your providers did testing and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. What to Expect at Home Because of possible injury to the brain from the stroke, you may notice problems with: Changes in behavior Doing easy tasks Memory Moving one side of the body Muscle spasms Paying attention Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Talking or understanding others Thinking Seeing to one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you used to do alone before the stroke. You may have physical or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common as you learn to live with the changes. It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Moving Around Moving around and doing normal tasks may be hard after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about making changes in your home to make it easier to do everyday activities. Find out about what you can do to prevent falls and keep your bathroom safe to use. السكتة الدماغية - الخروج من المستشفى كنت في المستشفى بعد إصابتك بسكتة دماغية. تحدث السكتة الدماغية عندما يتوقف تدفق الدم إلى جزء من الدماغ. في المنزل، اتبع تعليمات مقدم الرعاية الصحية حول العناية الذاتية. استخدم المعلومات أدناه كتذكير. عندما تكون في المستشفى أولاً، تلقيت علاجًا لمنع حدوث ضرر إضافي للدماغ، ولمساعدة القلب والرئتين والأعضاء المهمة الأخرى على التعافي. بعد أن أصبحت حالتك مستقرة، قام مقدمو الرعاية بإجراء فحوصات وبدأوا العلاج لمساعدتك على التعافي من السكتة الدماغية ومنع حدوث سكتة دماغية أخرى في المستقبل. قد تكون مكثت في وحدة خاصة تساعد الأشخاص على التعافي بعد السكتة الدماغية. ما يمكن توقعه في المنزل بسبب احتمال حدوث إصابة في الدماغ نتيجة السكتة الدماغية، قد تلاحظ مشكلات في: - تغيّرات في السلوك - القيام بالمهام السهلة - الذاكرة - تحريك أحد جانبي الجسم - تشنجات العضلات - الانتباه - الإحساس أو الوعي بجزء من الجسم - البلع - التحدث أو فهم الآخرين - التفكير - الرؤية في جهة واحدة (نصف عمى بصري - hemianopsia) قد تحتاج إلى مساعدة في الأنشطة اليومية التي كنت تقوم بها وحدك قبل السكتة الدماغية. قد تحصل على علاج طبيعي أو علاج وظيفي لاستعادة قدرتك على القيام بهذه الوظائف. الاكتئاب بعد السكتة الدماغية أمر شائع إلى حد ما، بينما تتعلم التعايش مع التغييرات. قد يظهر الاكتئاب بعد السكتة الدماغية مباشرة أو حتى بعد مرور سنتين على السكتة. لا تقد سيارتك بدون إذن من مقدم الرعاية الصحية. الحركة والتنقل قد يكون التحرك والقيام بالمهام العادية صعبًا بعد السكتة الدماغية. تأكد من أن منزلك آمن. اسأل مقدم الرعاية أو المعالج أو الممرضة عن التغييرات التي يمكن إجراؤها في المنزل لتسهيل القيام بالأنشطة اليومية. تعرّف إلى ما يمكنك فعله لمنع السقوط وجعل الحمام آمنًا للاستخدام. قد تحتاج الأسرة ومقدمو الرعاية إلى المساعدة في: - تمارين للحفاظ على ليونة المرفقين والكتفين والمفاصل الأخرى - مراقبة تيبّس المفاصل (التقلصات - contractures) - التأكد من استخدام الجبائر بالطريقة الصحيحة - التأكد من أن الذراعين والساقين في وضعية جيدة عند الجلوس أو الاستلقاء إذا كنت أنت أو من تحب يستخدم كرسيًا متحركًا، فإن زيارات المتابعة للتأكد من أن الكرسي مناسب بشكل جيد مهمة...

Back-Translation (to English):

Stroke – Discharge from the Hospital You were in the hospital after you had a stroke. A stroke happens when the blood flow to a part of the brain stops. At home, follow your health care provider’s instructions about self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When You Are in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent additional damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other important organs recover. After your condition became stable, the care providers performed tests and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent another stroke in the future. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. What to Expect at Home Because of the possibility of brain injury as a result of the stroke, you may notice problems with: - Changes in behavior - Doing easy tasks - Memory - Moving one side of the body - Muscle spasms - Attention - Sensation or awareness of a part of the body - Swallowing - Speaking or understanding others - Thinking - Seeing on one side (visual hemianopia – hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities that you used to do on your own before the stroke. You may receive physical therapy or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common, while you are learning to live with the changes. Depression may appear right after the stroke or even after two years have passed since the stroke. Do not drive your car without permission from your health care provider. Movement and Mobility Moving and doing normal tasks may be difficult after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about changes that can be made at home to make daily activities easier. Learn what you can do to prevent falls and make the bathroom safe to use. Family and caregivers may need to help with: - Exercises to keep the elbows, shoulders, and other joints flexible -...

# Stroke - Discharge from the Hospital You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke occurs when blood flow stops to part of the brain. At home, follow your healthcare provider's instructions regarding self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. ## When You Were in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help heal the heart, lungs, and other important organs. After your condition became stable, care providers performed tests and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. ## What to Expect at Home Because of the possible injury to the brain from the stroke, you may notice problems with: - Behavior changes - Doing easy tasks - Memory - Moving one side of the body - Muscle spasms - Attention - Sensation or awareness of one part of the body - Swallowing - Speaking or understanding others - Thinking - Vision to one side (hemianopia) You may need help with daily activities that you used to do alone before the stroke. You may undergo physical therapy or occupational therapy to regain your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common as you learn to live with the changes. It may develop a short period after the stroke or even two years after it. **Do not drive a car without permission from your healthcare provider.** ## Getting Around Getting around and doing normal tasks may be difficult after a stroke. Item: EVAL_0256 | Document: SURG_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. العناية بالجرح الجراحي - المفتوح الشق الجراحي هو قطع في الجلد يتم عمله أثناء الجراحة. ويُسمى أيضًا الجرح الجراحي. بعض الشقوق صغيرة، وأخرى طويلة. يعتمد حجم الشق على نوع العملية الجراحية التي خضعت لها. أحيانًا ينفتح الشق. قد يحدث هذا على طول كامل القطع أو في جزء منه فقط. قد يقرر الجرّاح عدم إغلاقه مرة أخرى بالغُرَز (الغُرَز الجراحية). ما يمكن توقعه في المنزل إذا لم يُغلق الجرّاح الجرح مرة أخرى بالغُرَز، فستحتاج إلى العناية به في المنزل، لأن التئامه قد يستغرق وقتًا. سيلتئم الجرح من الأسفل إلى الأعلى. يساعد الضماد على امتصاص الإفرازات ويمنع الجلد من الإغلاق قبل أن يمتلئ الجرح من الداخل. غسل اليدين بشكل صحيح من المهم تنظيف يديك قبل تغيير الضماد. يمكنك استخدام مطهّر كحولي لليدين. أو يمكنك غسل يديك باتباع الخطوات التالية: - انزع كل المجوهرات من يديك. - بلّل يديك، مع توجيههما إلى الأسفل تحت ماء جارٍ دافئ. - أضف الصابون واغسل يديك لمدة 15 إلى 30 ثانية (غنِّ أغنية "عيد ميلاد سعيد" أو "أغنية الحروف الأبجدية" مرة واحدة كاملة). نظّف تحت أظافرك أيضًا. - اشطف يديك جيدًا. - جففهما بمنشفة نظيفة. إزالة الضماد القديم سيخبرك مقدم الرعاية الصحية بعدد المرات التي يجب أن تغيّر فيها الضماد. للتحضير لتغيير الضماد: - نظّف يديك قبل لمس الضماد. - تأكد من أن جميع المستلزمات في متناول يدك. - جهّز سطح عمل نظيفًا. إزالة الضماد القديم: - فك الشريط اللاصق عن جلدك برفق. - استخدم قفازًا طبيًا نظيفًا (غير معقم) للإمساك بالضماد القديم ونزعه. - إذا التصق الضماد بالجرح، بلّله وحاول مرة أخرى، إلا إذا طلب منك مقدم الرعاية أن تنزعه وهو جاف. - ضع الضماد القديم في كيس بلاستيكي وضعه جانبًا. - نظّف يديك مرة أخرى بعد إزالة الضماد القديم. العناية بالجرح يمكنك استخدام شاش معقم أو قطعة قماش ناعمة لتنظيف الجلد حول الجرح: - استخدم محلول ملحي عادي (ماء وملح) أو ماءً مع صابون لطيف. - انقع الشاش أو القماش في المحلول الملحي أو الماء والصابون، ثم ربّت أو امسح الجلد برفق. - حاول إزالة كل الإفرازات وأي دم جاف أو مواد أخرى قد تكون تراكمت على الجلد. - لا تستخدم منظفات الجلد، أو الكحول، أو بيروكسيد الهيدروجين، أو اليود، أو الصابون الذي يحتوي على مواد مضادة للبكتيريا. هذه المواد يمكن أن تضر بأنسجة الجرح وتبطئ ...

Back-Translation (to English):

Care of the Surgical Wound – Open The surgical incision is a cut in the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called the surgical wound. Some incisions are small, and others are long. The size of the incision depends on the type of surgery you had. Sometimes the incision opens. This may happen along the entire length of the cut or only in part of it. The surgeon may decide not to close it again with stitches (surgical stitches). What to expect at home If the surgeon does not close the wound again with stitches, you will need to care for it at home, because its healing may take time. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. The dressing helps absorb the drainage and prevents the skin from closing before the wound is filled in from the inside. Proper handwashing It is important to clean your hands before changing the dressing. You can use an alcohol hand sanitizer. Or you can wash your hands by following these steps: - Remove all jewelry from your hands. - Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. - Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing the “Happy Birthday” song or the “Alphabet Song” one full time). Clean under your nails as well. - Rinse your hands well. - Dry them with a clean towel. Removing the old dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often you should change the dressing. To prepare for changing the dressing: - Clean your hands before touching the dressing. - Make sure all supplies are within reach. - Prepare a clean work surface. Removing the old dressing: - Gently loosen the adhesive tape from your skin. - Use a clean (non-sterile) medical glove to hold and remove the old dressing. - If the dressing sticks to the wound, moisten it and try again, unless your provider has asked you to remove it dry. - Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. - Clean your hands again after removing the old dressing. Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Hip Joint Replacement – Hospital Discharge Instructions You have undergone surgery to replace all or part of the hip joint with an artificial joint called a “prosthesis.” This article explains what you need to do to care for your new hip joint after you leave the hospital. When you are in the hospital You have undergone hip replacement surgery to replace all or part of the hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to expect at home When you return home, you should be able to walk using a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Ask your surgeon when it is appropriate to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help, and be able to get in and out of bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful so that the artificial hip joint does not come out of place (dislocation of the joint), especially in the first months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that strengthen the new hip joint, and take special precautions. You will need someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after leaving the hospital or rehabilitation center. You will need help preparing food, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your previous level of activity. But you will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or violent sports like American football and football (soccer). But you should be able to do low‑impact activities such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and playing golf. Arranging the home Your bed should be low enough that your feet touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Hip Replacement - Discharge from the Hospital Surgery was performed to replace all or part of the hip joint with an artificial joint called a "prosthesis". This article tells you what you must do to care for your new hip upon leaving the hospital. When you are in the hospital Hip replacement surgery was performed to replace all or part of the hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a "prosthesis". What to expect at home By the time you return home, you should be able to walk using a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks pass. Check with your surgeon regarding when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only simple help, and be able to get on and off your bed or chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful not to dislocate the artificial hip joint, especially in the first months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for a week to two weeks after leaving the hospital or rehabilitation center. You will need help with preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your previous level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or sports that require physical contact like American football and soccer. But you should be able to do low-impact activities, such as hiking, gardening (garden works), swimming, playing tennis, and golf. Preparing the home Your bed must be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed must also be high enough for your hips to be higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress must be firm. Item: EVAL_0294 | Document: CARD_001 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? Heart failure - Leaving the hospital Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to efficiently pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body. When symptoms become acute, staying in the hospital may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When you are in the hospital You were in the hospital to receive treatment for heart failure. Heart failure occurs when your heart muscles are weak or have difficulty relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that transports fluids throughout your body. As is the case with any pump, if the outflow from the pump is not sufficient, the fluids do not move well and accumulate in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluids collect in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team carefully adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also monitored and measured the amount of urine you produced. You may have received medications to help your body get rid of excess fluids. You may have had tests done to check how well your heart is working. What to expect at home Your energy will return slowly. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first return home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Self-examination at home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you wake up - before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothes each time you weigh yourself. Write your weight every day on a chart so you can track it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: • Is my energy level normal? • Do I feel more shortness of breath when I do my daily activities? • Have my clothes or shoes become tight? • Are my ankles or legs swollen? • Am I coughing more frequently? Does my cough sound wet? • Do I feel shortness of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are experiencing new (or differ...

Item: EVAL_0302 | Document: CARD_002 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...
